  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_5768a82d-25dd-4991-a2bf-f34fd351af7a'
      });

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer283591227768900416">The dryly precocious, soon-to-be-fifteen-year-old hero of this engagingly offbeat debut novel, Oliver Tate lives in the seaside town of Swansea, Wales. At once a self-styled social scientist, a spy in the baffling adult world surrounding him, and a budding, hormone-driven emotional explorer, Oliver is stealthily (and perhaps a bit more nervously than he’d ever admit) nosin</span>
  <span id="freeText283591227768900416" style="display:none">The dryly precocious, soon-to-be-fifteen-year-old hero of this engagingly offbeat debut novel, Oliver Tate lives in the seaside town of Swansea, Wales. At once a self-styled social scientist, a spy in the baffling adult world surrounding him, and a budding, hormone-driven emotional explorer, Oliver is stealthily (and perhaps a bit more nervously than he’d ever admit) nosing his way forward through the murky and uniquely perilous waters of adolescence. His objectives? Uncovering the secrets behind his parents’ teetering marriage, unraveling the mystery that is his alluring and equally quirky classmate Jordana Bevan, and understanding where he fits in among the pansexuals, Zoroastrians, and other mystifying, fascinating beings in his orbit.<br /><br />“It’s in my interests to know about my parents’ mental problems,” he reasons. Thus, when he discovers that his affable dad is quietly struggling with depression, Oliver marshals all the daytime-TV pop-psychology wisdom at his command–not to mention his formidable, uninhibited powers of imagination–in order to put things right again. But a covert expedition into the mysterious territory of middle-aged malaise is bound to be tricky business for a teenager with more to learn about the agonies and ecstasies of life than a pocket thesaurus and his “worldly” school chum Chips can teach him. <br /><br />Ready or not, however, Oliver is about to get a crash course. His awkwardly torrid and tender relationship with Jordana is hurtling at the speed of teenage passion toward the inevitable magic moment . . . and whatever lies beyond. And his boy-detective exploits have set him on a collision course with the New Age old flame who’s resurfaced in his mother’s life to lead her into temptation with lessons in surfing, self-defense . . . and maybe seduction. Struggling to buoy his parents’ wedded bliss, deep-six his own virginity, and sound the depths of heartache, happiness, and the business of being human, what’s a lad to do? Poised precariously on the cusp of innocence and experience, yesterday’s daydreams and tomorrow’s decisions, Oliver Tate aims to damn the torpedoes and take the plunge.</span>
  <a data-text-id="283591227768900416" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      </div>

    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div id="review_283842218" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/283842218" />
    <a title="Jo" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2467655-jo"><img alt="Jo" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1596119990p2/2467655.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/283842218?book_show_action=true">Feb 25, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jo" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jo" href="/user/show/2467655-jo">Jo</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer283842218" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7759544084624819799">Ever wondered what it would be like if Wes Anderson got drunk on vodka and watched the entire box set of The Inbetweeners in one night?<br/><br/>Reader meet <i>Submarine</i>. <i>Submarine </i>meet reader.<br/><br/>Aah, and herein lies the conundrum.<br/>For I really dislike The Inbetweeners (I know, I know… I have received many a horrified glare when I have divulged this information. I just don’t find it funny because I’m a horrible, stuffy prude) <i>but</i> I adore Wes Anderson.<br/>This could have gone either way and I think I knew this risk b</span>
  <span id="freeText7759544084624819799" style="display:none">Ever wondered what it would be like if Wes Anderson got drunk on vodka and watched the entire box set of The Inbetweeners in one night?<br /><br />Reader meet <i>Submarine</i>. <i>Submarine </i>meet reader.<br /><br />Aah, and herein lies the conundrum.<br />For I really dislike The Inbetweeners (I know, I know… I have received many a horrified glare when I have divulged this information. I just don’t find it funny because I’m a horrible, stuffy prude) <i>but</i> I adore Wes Anderson.<br />This could have gone either way and I think I knew this risk before I started this book. I am nothing if not a risk taker.<br /><br />So, let’s see shall we?<br />Did I enjoy this book?<br />Very much so.<br />Did I laugh at this book?<br />I barked like a seal a few times, so yes, again… very much so.<br />Is it a bit vile and vulgar and is there lots of graphic sex and fumblings?<br />Ho yes.<br /><br />Considering I only read this book so I had something to talk to Richard Ayoade about (I’m kidding. Our <s>imaginary</s> conversations would be about lots of excellent things from films to music, to whether it would make him uncomfortable if I touched his hair), I really, <i>really</i> enjoyed it. <br />I like to call books like these “It got dark” books; meaning you pick it up in the afternoon and you read…and read….and read and then the next time you look up well, um, it got dark. <br />This is exactly what happened with<i> Submarine</i>. <br />My <s>inhaling</s> reading of Mr Dunthorne’s debut was only hindered occasionally by me stopping to jot down some of my favourite quotes and then pick up my phone so I could text them to my sister. <br /><br />I battled (well ok, maybe battled is getting a bit giddy) with the decision to whether I should class this as young adult. I know that just because a book has a fifteen year old protagonist doesn’t automatically make it a young adult book. Like I said before, this book has lots and lots of awkward sexual shenanigans in and frank discussions about things that might not be to everyone’s liking. So if you’re put off by that, maybe give this book a miss or at least approach with caution. <br />But, even though people might argue with me saying it’s not a young adult book, I believe if an author writes a book where there is a fifteen year old narrator and it’s a realistic portrayal (which I personally feel Mr Dunthorne managed brilliantly), then why shouldn’t a fifteen year old pick the book up? <br /><br />Plus, classing this book as YA would make it <i>infinitely</i> cooler because YA fiction is all the rage right now and all the indie kids read it and pretend they liked it before it got famous. <br />(FYI, we hard core YAddicts read Hunger Games before it was cool)<br />It’s kind of like I’m doing Mr D a favour reviewing this on a YA blog. I’m sure he appreciates it. <br /><br />*cough*<br /><br />Anyway, I loved Oliver. I’d probably <i>hate</i> him if I met him in real life, though. If we had gone to high school with each other I would have watched him cautiously from behind my ill-advised fringe and think that he was odd and trouble. He’d be the kind of guy I’d cross the road to avoid passing… but I’d be <i>intrigued</i> by him. <br />I understand that that probably says more about me than Oliver, but that’s kind of how I felt about him in this book. I wouldn’t say I necessarily liked him as a character, but he was a brilliant narrator. I loved seeing the world through his warped-tinted spectacles. <br /><br />Some of my favourite Oliver quotes, which I have realised won’t be funny out of context: <br /><br />[On pocket-sized Encyclopaedias]<i> “It would only fit in a pocket that was specially designed.”</i><br /><i>
  <br />
</i><br /><i>“I slam my fist on to the table to no effect. It’s made of stone.” </i><br /><i>
  <br />
</i><br /><i>“I would never say snog. I would say osculate.”</i><br /><br />[On condoms] <i>“The smell nothing like a positive first sexual experience.” </i><br /><br />I find that a book like this is incredibly difficult to describe why it’s funny to people because if you don’t find it funny, then no matter how many times I say <i> “specially designed pockets”</i> and cackle, you will still look at me blankly and wonder why we are friends.<br />But <i>I</i> laughed. <br /><br />I don’t really want to go into the story because, if I’m honest, there’s not <i>that</i> much of a story. It’s about a boy growing up in South Wales where things aren’t always peachy and the boy observes them with dry humour. <br /><br />The only gripe I had with this book is that I wish certain parts had been explored more in depth. There were some things that seemed to either get overwhelmed by Oliver’s personality/narration, mentioned a few times and then forgotten about or, at the end, solved and wrapped up in a neat bow.<br /><br /><b>Theme Tune.</b><br /><a href="http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=vSwpLkpJ45w" rel="nofollow noopener">Hiding Tonight by Alex Turner.;</a><br /><br />It’s like this song was written for this story.<br /><br />Heh… heh…. <br />Actually, that um… <i>joke </i>would have been funnier if this song <i>had</i> been written for this story. But, it’s not true because I think Mr Turner wrote these songs before the film soundtrack, so I’m not just unfunny; I’m also a liar. <br />I couldn’t find the perfect song for this book but I love this song and… wait, why am I even justifying myself? It’s Alex Bloody Turner.<br /><br />Also um… with regards to Welsh Week. I’m… um, sure that the Arctic Monkeys are from Sheffield which is in England which is part of the UK along wiiiiiiiith….. Wales!<br />Also, I’m sure they played in Wales at some point and really enjoyed it.<br /><br />*cough*<br /><br />I really enjoyed this book but I know that it won’t be for everyone. It’s clever, it’s witty, it’s occasionally vulgar and it’s definitely a hipster’s paradise (which is <i>nothing</i> like a gangsta’s paradise… something I found out recently. But that’s another story for another time.)<br /><br />I’ll let you make up your own mind whether that’s a good thing or not.<br /><br /><br />This review is part of <a href="http://www.weartheoldcoat.com/2012/02/wythnos-cymraeg-welsh-week.html" rel="nofollow noopener">Wythnos Cymraeg || Welsh Week</a>. Also it comes with a <a href="http://www.weartheoldcoat.com/2012/02/submarine-joe-dunthorne-and-film-review.html" rel="nofollow noopener">snazzy film review</a>.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7759544084624819799" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer14832896755242710398">One of those few cases where the film is much better than the book. :-(<br/><br/>Don't get me wrong, I loved the 2010 film <i>Submarine</i>, it was an amazing experience to watch. But the book? Well, I'm not much of a fan of pervy sex jokes or fart jokes, and at least the film spares viewers of those for the most part. The book had a number of lines that made absolutely no sense, trying so hard to be obscure and intelligent that it failed to get to any sort of point. It has its moments of true humor and good wri</span>
  <span id="freeText14832896755242710398" style="display:none">One of those few cases where the film is much better than the book. :-(<br /><br />Don't get me wrong, I loved the 2010 film <i>Submarine</i>, it was an amazing experience to watch. But the book? Well, I'm not much of a fan of pervy sex jokes or fart jokes, and at least the film spares viewers of those for the most part. The book had a number of lines that made absolutely no sense, trying so hard to be obscure and intelligent that it failed to get to any sort of point. It has its moments of true humor and good writing, a few good quotes here and there, but mostly it was just overly bizarre, and not in a good way.</span>

<div id="review_1041332961" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1041332961" />
    <a title="Algernon (Darth Anyan)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4728718-algernon-darth-anyan"><img alt="Algernon (Darth Anyan)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1553101944p2/4728718.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1041332961?book_show_action=true">Aug 31, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Algernon (Darth Anyan)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Algernon (Darth Anyan)" href="/user/show/4728718-algernon-darth-anyan">Algernon (Darth Anyan)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780812978391" href="/book/show/7715769-submarine">review of another edition</a>


          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Algernon (Darth Anyan) by: </span>
      saw the movie
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4728718-algernon-darth-anyan?shelf=2014">2014</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1041332961" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18253497659466024829"><br/>Things I've learned from Submarine (I planned to list 100, but I got tired of the game rather fast):<br/><br/>- you are a triskaidekaphobic if you are afraid of the number thirteen<br/><br/>- it's OK to spy on your parents in order to find out things about yourself (<i> I recently discovered that my mother has been typing the names of as-yet-uninvented mental conditions into Yahoo's search engine: 'delusion syndrome teenage', 'over-active imagination problem', 'holistic behavioural stabilizers' </i><br/><br/>- a nepenthe is somethi</span>
  <span id="freeText18253497659466024829" style="display:none"><br />Things I've learned from Submarine (I planned to list 100, but I got tired of the game rather fast):<br /><br />- you are a triskaidekaphobic if you are afraid of the number thirteen<br /><br />- it's OK to spy on your parents in order to find out things about yourself (<i> I recently discovered that my mother has been typing the names of as-yet-uninvented mental conditions into Yahoo's search engine: 'delusion syndrome teenage', 'over-active imagination problem', 'holistic behavioural stabilizers' </i><br /><br />- a nepenthe is something that helps you forget sorrow and suffering, like a bottle of poppers.<br /><br />- sometimes it is important to skip school for an afternoon (this might be a nod to Ferris Bueller)<br /><br />- cooking and love-making are, after all, interchangeable skills<br /><br />- it is generally true that 15 years old are obsessed with sex (<i> I've discovered that masturbating in the darkness of my empty wardrobe is excellent, particularly because of that new-born feeling as you stumble back into the well-lit room. A kind of Narnia. </i>)<br /><br />- in related news : a parthenologist is a specialist in the study of virgins and virginity.<br /><br />- love means that <i> she's the only person I would allow to be shrunk to microscopic size and explore my body in a tiny submersible machine. </i><br /><br />- every human eats six spiders a year while asleep.<br /><br />- gross out humour is the number one entertainment for the younger generations : <i> Out in the bay, the Cork ferry may look like civilization but it probably contains at least one person vomiting. </i><br /><br />- paruresis is the fear of peeing in public places<br /><br />- there's a place called Llanwrthwl somewhere in Wales. (and that's probably one of the easier to pronounce names from the country)<br /><br />- people from Cardiff are closer to apes than the rest of the members of the human race.<br /><br />- meditation is like a long bath<br /><br />- an egregore is a kind of group mind which is created when people consciously come together for a common purpose.<br /><br />- to exungulate means to trim or cut nails or hoofs.<br /><br />- <i> Car journeys are the frowning parentheses at the start and end of any good holiday. </i> (this quote is actually one of the really good ones in the novel)<br /><br />- syzygy means the alignment of three celestial objects.<br /><br />Some of these may be made up words, but I didn't care enough one way or another to check up on them. Ollie, the 15 y.o. who writes in his journal all this stuff, is real keen on dictionaries and likes to show off to all and sundry how clever he is. He is, of course, also self-obssessed (<b> 'Why don't we talk about me?' </b>), a bully, sex-obsessed, hypochondriac, a self-serving liar, bored, eccentric and interesting in his own eyes, unnecessarily gross and annoying by the end of the story to me.<br /><br />It's a generational thing I guess, and the fact that I don't appreciate the toilet humour, the vomiting and the farts and the general ickiness of the presentation makes me an old-fart in the eyes of the younger generation. I could make comparison between Submarine and such classics as The Catcher In The Rye, The Graduate, Ferris Bueller's Day Off, Superbad. The novel aspires to such lofty ideals. Olliver is the Swansea translation of the angsty, smart, sensitive teenager who hides his vulnerability by lashing out at the people around him.<br /><br />After the first couple of chapters, I was captivated by the humour and the style of presentation, but the story got old quite fast when I realized that's all there is to it - more style than substance and too much reliance on being shocking and trending (1990's Yuppie style). I believe there are some serious pacing issues and the novel goes on too long, especially for those readers who become disenchanted with the first person narrator. I saw the movie before reading the book, and the same thing happened there : I loved the first half hour, and then fell asleep when it kept going nowhere in the next five hours (I know the movie is shorter than that, but that's how it felt).<br /><br />That's not to say other readers will not be more susceptible to the charms of Ollie and his brand of humour. I thought the girls in the book where well rendered, both the pyromaniac Jordana and the bullied Eve. I also think some of the issues of bullying in school, communication between generations and the way parent marital troubles reflect on the sanity of the children are worthy subjects of analysis. And I believe Joe Dunthorne has it in him to be a great modern writer. Here's a last quote to illustrate both that he can write, and what the book is all about:<br /><br /><i> This is theatre. It feels like this could be some clever extra scene from the play, and in a minute there's going to be a song about how lucky we all are to be young and beautiful and live in Swansea at the end of the less awful half of an absolute bum-out of a century. </i><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="18253497659466024829" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_339665725" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/339665725" />
    <a title="Toby" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5454475-toby"><img alt="Toby" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1542247337p2/5454475.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/339665725?book_show_action=true">May 30, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Toby" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Toby" href="/user/show/5454475-toby">Toby</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780241955154" href="/book/show/10789096-submarine">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5454475-toby?shelf=lit">lit</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer339665725" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2795692162142722433">Maybe it's just me but doesn't <i>everything</i> get compared to <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/5107.The_Catcher_in_the_Rye' title='The Catcher in the Rye by J.D. Salinger' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Catcher in the Rye</a>? A modern day version, a version crossed with Godzilla, Holden Caulfield on speed, best thing since sliced Salinger? I choose to discount these comparisons for three reasons: hyperbole, im always disappointed <b>and</b> most importantly, I distinctly remember finally reading it and wondering what all the fuss was about.<br/><br/>Joe Dunthorne's debut novel about teenage angst, alienation and rebellion in Swansea in the late 1990's w</span>
  <span id="freeText2795692162142722433" style="display:none">Maybe it's just me but doesn't <i>everything</i> get compared to <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/5107.The_Catcher_in_the_Rye" title="The Catcher in the Rye by J.D. Salinger" rel="nofollow noopener">The Catcher in the Rye</a>? A modern day version, a version crossed with Godzilla, Holden Caulfield on speed, best thing since sliced Salinger? I choose to discount these comparisons for three reasons: hyperbole, im always disappointed <b>and</b> most importantly, I distinctly remember finally reading it and wondering what all the fuss was about.<br /><br />Joe Dunthorne's debut novel about teenage angst, alienation and rebellion in Swansea in the late 1990's was inevitably compared to said adventures of Master Caulfield and once more I find myself wondering what all the fuss is about.<br /><br />There are moments of great levity interspersed with others of great gravity, both handled well considering this is a book that features a teenage boy inadvertantly trying to put his fist inside a virgin in a theatre sound booth and same boy writing awkward teenage love letters to his mother from his father in an attempt to get them to "<i>turn the dimmer switch down</i>" on their bedroom light.<br /><br />It's quite a charming novel of coming to terms with yourself and your parents and sadly those themes and the age of the protagonist means this does limp in to the dreaded young adult category of literature. Whilst <i>Catcher</i> has become known as a book for teens it was intended for adults and as such deals with things beyond the school yard as it investigates it's issues of identity and alienation, <i>Subamrine</i> gets bogged down in the minutiae of bodily fluids, obvious attempts at making Oliver a modern day <i>picaro</i> but forgetting to make him sympathetic in any way at all. At its heart this is immaturity masquerading as mature, a wolf dressed in the sheeps clothing of <i>a large dictionary, thesaurus and encyclopedia</i> set, perfectly mirroring its protagonist.<br /><br />I must be honest and admit that I only had an interest in reading this because of the fantastic movie adaptation. It's such a charming, funny and interesting movie and I was intrigued as to whether the novel was the source for this or the film maker <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/4468597.Richard_Ayoade" title="Richard Ayoade" rel="nofollow noopener">Richard Ayoade</a>. The answer was a little from column A and a lot from column B. The adaptation made this novel better, it added a magical quality missing from (OK maybe it was slightly hinted at) Dunthornes prose, made the story of Oliver Tate more cohesive and most importantly adding layers of sympathy to the supporting characters in the life of this neurotic, self obsessed, teenage boy and even Oliver himself.<br /><br /><b>See the movie, for once book not essential.</b></span>
  <a data-text-id="2795692162142722433" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_179483162" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/179483162" />
    <a title="Charlie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5085835-charlie"><img alt="Charlie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1344938813p2/5085835.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/179483162?book_show_action=true">Jun 25, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Charlie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Charlie" href="/user/show/5085835-charlie">Charlie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">boys aged around 15, teenagers who don't always fit in with their peers</span></div>

        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer179483162" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16220288471407379626">I picked this up in a bookstore because it was at a discounted price and the first few pages really caught my attention. Oliver Tate as a smart albeit eccentric teenager struck a chord with me, and at first I felt I could relate to the character. I enjoyed the character's analytical view of the world around him and the humour that comes with it, but that's about where it ends.<br/><br/>Oliver Tate does things which most teenagers may threaten to do after an argument, but would never dream of doing in real</span>
  <span id="freeText16220288471407379626" style="display:none">I picked this up in a bookstore because it was at a discounted price and the first few pages really caught my attention. Oliver Tate as a smart albeit eccentric teenager struck a chord with me, and at first I felt I could relate to the character. I enjoyed the character's analytical view of the world around him and the humour that comes with it, but that's about where it ends.<br /><br />Oliver Tate does things which most teenagers may threaten to do after an argument, but would never dream of doing in real life. It's simply unrealistic. Also, despite his unique personality, he surprisingly fits in very well at school and has no problems hiding his true self in order to gain popularity, which is something that a real teenager with a similar sort of personality (ie. myself) would struggle immensley to do.<br /><br />A lot of the language and situations presented in the book are extremely vulgar and graphic. Whilst it may be unsuitable for any younger teenagers, I doubt that the book would appeal to adults either due to the protagonist being a 15-year-old boy at odds with his parents. I had hoped that a character with Oliver's personality would not also be subject to stereotypical teenage, loutish behaviours. I really began to lose faith in him, especially about half way through the book where Oliver does something which would be seen as criminal and foolish under any circumstances.<br /><br />The plot is pretty non-existent, dependant only on Oliver's increasingly idiotic behaviours to push it forwards. Expect a typical story written from a rebellious teenager's eyes, full of issues faced by teenagers and their friends and family. Overall, it is an easy read with some humour, but not much else.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16220288471407379626" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer160255711" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5421304981689415347">Oliver Tate is a Welsh teenager with a penchant for theatrics and complicated words. He is our narrator. <br/><br/>Our author, Joe Dunthorne, punctuates his book with clever turns of phrases, keeps his sentences succinct and uses natural phrases that flow together well. His writing is fantastic. He gives Oliver such a unique voice, making this entertaining and easy to read. <br/><br/>But there's an errant sadness that runs through this book. Oliver is coming of age and realizing that he cannot control his life--tha</span>
  <span id="freeText5421304981689415347" style="display:none">Oliver Tate is a Welsh teenager with a penchant for theatrics and complicated words. He is our narrator. <br /><br />Our author, Joe Dunthorne, punctuates his book with clever turns of phrases, keeps his sentences succinct and uses natural phrases that flow together well. His writing is fantastic. He gives Oliver such a unique voice, making this entertaining and easy to read. <br /><br />But there's an errant sadness that runs through this book. Oliver is coming of age and realizing that he cannot control his life--that no one, not even his parents, can control their lives.<br /><br />The sadness doesn't dominate the text though. It highlights the hilarity and sometimes frivolous nature of life, especially Oliver's.<br /><br />The following are two of my favorite quotes (which are almost impossible to understand out of context--another reason to pick this book up!):<br /><br />"And it was strange being his wife for a while; it was nice that he was being so open and I liked hearing him swear, but I can't say that, after a few weeks of listening to him moan, I didn't see the appeal--theoretically--of running off with the guy who comes once a month to do the garden."<br /><br />"She's the only person I would allow to be shrunk to microscopic size and explore my body in a tiny submersible machine." </span>
  <a data-text-id="5421304981689415347" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_33656583" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/33656583" />
    <a title="Ciara" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1333177-ciara"><img alt="Ciara" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442487190p2/1333177.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/33656583?book_show_action=true">Sep 23, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ciara" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ciara" href="/user/show/1333177-ciara">Ciara</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">stalk-y teenage boys, people who loathe dogs, teenagers obsessed with losing their virginities</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1333177-ciara?shelf=read-in-2008">read-in-2008</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer33656583" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3830762337524815512">i found this book tremdeously disappointing. it caught my eye at the bookstore, i guess because of the dust jacket. i'm a sucker for design, seriously. a jacket can make or break a book for me (see my review of <u>ruined by reading</u> for another example). however, i was smart & i exercised restraint. i left the book sitting on the shelf & got a copy from the library instead. thank god! it's the story if a disaffected welsh teenager, a boy. he suspects that his parents are having marital troubles, & t</span>
  <span id="freeText3830762337524815512" style="display:none">i found this book tremdeously disappointing. it caught my eye at the bookstore, i guess because of the dust jacket. i'm a sucker for design, seriously. a jacket can make or break a book for me (see my review of <u>ruined by reading</u> for another example). however, i was smart &amp; i exercised restraint. i left the book sitting on the shelf &amp; got a copy from the library instead. thank god! it's the story if a disaffected welsh teenager, a boy. he suspects that his parents are having marital troubles, &amp; that his mother might be pursuing an affair with her meditation guru. he has also found himself a girlfriend &amp; manages to get her into the sack, so he is obsessing over the fact that he lost his virginity while he obsesses over his parents' love/sex life, even going so far as to count his mother's tampons to make sure she doesn't get pregnant. i think that is where the book truly lost me. who does that? i'm all for demystifying the human body &amp; wish that more dudes were comfortable with the fact that ladies tend to menstruate, but counting one's own mother's tampons really crosses a line for me. i can't craft an eloquent explanation as to why, but nonetheless. plus he runs kind of hot &amp; cold with his girlfriend ("treat her mean, keep her keen" is his mantra...along with all teenage boys throughout the history of the world, which is why i just didn't bother dating dudes until i was in my 20s), who finally wises up &amp; dumps him. there's a scene where he kills his girlfriend's dog too. he seems to think it's a kind-hearted act, some kind of euthenasia, because his girlfriend is allergic to the dog, but he poisons it with fertilizer &amp; it's just really sick &amp; unnecessary. oh, &amp; he stalks his mom to a meditation center to make sure she's not boning her guru. &amp; does the same thing when his mom goes on a meditation trip to the beach. i'm just not into stalk-y teenage boys, sorry. i guess this is a kind of updated <u>youth in revolt</u>, another book that was lauded as being an honest protrait of precocious teenage male sexuality, but just left me feeling kind of ill. </span>
  <a data-text-id="3830762337524815512" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer2532954275" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer5315123520243284045"><b>2.5 Stars.</b><br/><br/>I was enjoying this, but then it went downhill quickly; I have quite a few issues with this one.<br/><br/>This book had a bit of <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/22628.The_Perks_of_Being_a_Wallflower' title='The Perks of Being a Wallflower by Stephen Chbosky' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Perks of Being a Wallflower</a> and <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/51070.The_Secret_Diary_of_Adrian_Mole__Aged_13_3_4__Adrian_Mole___1_' title='The Secret Diary of Adrian Mole, Aged 13 3/4 (Adrian Mole, #1) by Sue Townsend' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Secret Diary of Adrian Mole, Aged 13 3/4</a> with a bit of The Inbetweeners; that's not necessarily a bad mix, but it didn't feel very original.<br/><br/>The characters were really inconsistent and unlikable; one chapter they were acting and talking a certain way, and the next they felt like a different person. I just couldn't like them. There</span>
  <span id="freeText5315123520243284045" style="display:none"><b>2.5 Stars.</b><br /><br />I was enjoying this, but then it went downhill quickly; I have quite a few issues with this one.<br /><br />This book had a bit of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/22628.The_Perks_of_Being_a_Wallflower" title="The Perks of Being a Wallflower by Stephen Chbosky" rel="nofollow noopener">The Perks of Being a Wallflower</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/51070.The_Secret_Diary_of_Adrian_Mole__Aged_13_3_4__Adrian_Mole___1_" title="The Secret Diary of Adrian Mole, Aged 13 3/4 (Adrian Mole, #1) by Sue Townsend" rel="nofollow noopener">The Secret Diary of Adrian Mole, Aged 13 3/4</a> with a bit of The Inbetweeners; that's not necessarily a bad mix, but it didn't feel very original.<br /><br />The characters were really inconsistent and unlikable; one chapter they were acting and talking a certain way, and the next they felt like a different person. I just couldn't like them. There was also this shy fat girl called Zoe who was bullied in one school, so moved school, lost allllll the weight and suddenly grew huge boobs, and then she became this desirable more popular girl who leads boys on; yeah, not really happy about that, apparently the only way to be confident is to be skinny. There was a really descriptive scene of a dying dog that was <i>completely</i> unnecessary, it didn't have to happen.<br /><br />This started out as a light, enjoyable enough story with decent writing but now it's just a mess of annoying characters and ridiculous scenes. Not the worst book ever, but certainly not great.</span>

  <span id="freeText354612325747794981" style="display:none">I read this in a matter of days, closed up in my stink hole (sink hole) of a room, coughing violently and slathering vaseline on my peeling lips.       <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Michael" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Michael" href="/user/show/2099671-michael">Michael</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780141032757" href="/book/show/6328365-submarine">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2099671-michael?shelf=review">review</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2099671-michael?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2099671-michael?shelf=2010">2010</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer95961052" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17621101921830438292">Review from <a href='http://finxy.blogspot.com/2010/04/submarine.html' rel='nofollow noopener'>Badelynge</a><br/>Maybe if Joe Dunthorne's Submarine had clothed its covers with far fewer off the mark testimonials, I would have been a little more forgiving in my judgment of this book. But for the sake of balance alone somebody has to pooh-pooh all the best thing since Catcher in the Rye statements. To live up to such statements Oliver Tate (our narrator) would have to seem like a real character - but he never does. Maybe he was never meant to. Submarine sort of lives in a skewed reality </span>
  <span id="freeText17621101921830438292" style="display:none">Review from <a href="http://finxy.blogspot.com/2010/04/submarine.html" rel="nofollow noopener">Badelynge</a><br />Maybe if Joe Dunthorne's Submarine had clothed its covers with far fewer off the mark testimonials, I would have been a little more forgiving in my judgment of this book. But for the sake of balance alone somebody has to pooh-pooh all the best thing since Catcher in the Rye statements. To live up to such statements Oliver Tate (our narrator) would have to seem like a real character - but he never does. Maybe he was never meant to. Submarine sort of lives in a skewed reality not far removed from a post watershed episode of My Family. Other times it's hard to believe Oliver's ramblings are anything other than the voice of the true author, Joe Dunthorne. To be fair the first chapter was ok. It seemed quite light, quirky, with some pretty clever lines: 'Depression comes in bouts. Like boxing. Dad is in the blue corner.' Unfortunately that line was the last of them and even that one had been wasted on a cover quote. Are there any truths uncovered in this book, other than suggesting that 15 year olds aren't always as right as they think they are? Back to those pesky testimonials. No, no, no. 'Adrian Mole for adults, with a much more complicated protagonist, truer to life and infinitely funnier' Big Issue. I think somebody should go back and read the Adrian Mole books again, because this couldn't be further from the truth if Oliver Tate had written the quote himself. </span>
  <a data-text-id="17621101921830438292" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3236551024" />
    <a title="Negi Neguaien" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/90966978-negi-neguaien"><img alt="Negi Neguaien" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1552773389p2/90966978.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3236551024?book_show_action=true">Mar 20, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Negi Neguaien" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Negi Neguaien" href="/user/show/90966978-negi-neguaien">Negi Neguaien</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780241955154" href="/book/show/10789096-submarine">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3236551024" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer250037843615888733">Wonderful character study, awful story. <br/><br/>This book suffers from what I will now call "Dear Evan Hansen Syndrome", in which the main character is super interesting but morally unreliable and generally unlikeable, and there is no other likable character in the book (aside from this one throwaway boyfriend character, I guess, he had a few moral brain cells). There is no one to root for, so in a book that absolutely values characters over plot, my heartstrings are left rusty. <br/><br/>I read till the end in d</span>
  <span id="freeText250037843615888733" style="display:none">Wonderful character study, awful story. <br /><br />This book suffers from what I will now call "Dear Evan Hansen Syndrome", in which the main character is super interesting but morally unreliable and generally unlikeable, and there is no other likable character in the book (aside from this one throwaway boyfriend character, I guess, he had a few moral brain cells). There is no one to root for, so in a book that absolutely values characters over plot, my heartstrings are left rusty. <br /><br />I read till the end in desperate hopes that Joe Dunthorne could somehow pull Oliver Tate's story together, make him really come of age, make him learn that his self-taught quirkiness (or near-sociopathic behavior) did not make him superior, maybe even teach him that perverted humor, fat-shaming, and bullying would get him nowhere in life. <br /><br />This book was just uncomfortable to read after you get past the initial intrigue of Oliver, his manic views, and strange vocabulary. When you learn of the ways he sees women, overweight people, and his parents' sex life, it's all just gross to read. Graphic sex scenes were described dryly and distastefully, which might have been fine if they had a point: to show that physical intimacy does not match emotional intimacy or whatnot, but the scenes were just there to show how quirky of a guy Oliver is. <br /><br />This story is so... white. There is no positive representation of any minority. Zoe, an overweight girl, is described with disgusting imagery. Oliver is as privileged as can be. No woman could be seen in more than two dimensions. Pansexuality was used as a distasteful gag, homosexuality as a derogatory term.<br /><br />Over the top of it all, the writing is just bad. There is too much telling and not enough showing, using phrases like "I see", "I imagine", "I think" over and over, maybe because it's Oliver's character quirk but it just makes for poor literature. Joe Dunthorne values style over content, so there are always small unnecessary details. The book is also just too long. <br /><br />It was funny for the first ten pages, but overall, my funny bone is left untickled. Joe Dunthorne took some risks, and for that, I give two stars out of pity and desperation for more morally challenging characters.</span>
  <a data-text-id="250037843615888733" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Savannah Smith" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Savannah Smith" href="/user/show/5149575-savannah-smith">Savannah Smith</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

<span id="freeTextContainer6866455687195508">Oliver Tate is the most interesting character that I've ever read. Hands down. <br/><br/>I loved the way he thought in words. I loved his "words of the day". I smiled whenever I knew what one of the words meant, and it was nice to learn a new word when I didn't. I loved the British school boy humor and language. <br/><br/>I loved his way of thinking, even if it was extremely twisted at times. I love how he said everything that came across his mind, and it was so truly narrated. It was so nice to read a book where y</span>
  <span id="freeText6866455687195508" style="display:none">Oliver Tate is the most interesting character that I've ever read. Hands down. <br /><br />I loved the way he thought in words. I loved his "words of the day". I smiled whenever I knew what one of the words meant, and it was nice to learn a new word when I didn't. I loved the British school boy humor and language. <br /><br />I loved his way of thinking, even if it was extremely twisted at times. I love how he said everything that came across his mind, and it was so truly narrated. It was so nice to read a book where you really felt that you were reaching inside every part of the narrator's mind, not just the superficial part. I loved how he would explain how he was going to so do something for a certain reaction, and then go and do it. <br /><br />I loved Joe Dunthorne's writing. It was witty, exciting, and hilarious. He really knows how to tell a story well. <br /><br />My only (minor, very minor) complaint is that the middle of the book felt like it dragged a bit. Or that could be because of a reading lul I had while half-way through the book. But it wasn't enough of a problem for me to downgrade the book to four starts or to not consider it a favorite. <br /><br />I'm now excited to see the movie and see what Richard Ayoade did with such a lovely book. I also can't wait to hear Alex Turner's (yay!) amazing soundtrack (that I first heard back in march) in action with the film. <br /><br />Great book overall. Oliver Tate, I'm going to miss you!</span>
  <a data-text-id="6866455687195508" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

        rated it

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/563794-amy?shelf=2019-books-read">2019-books-read</a>,

<span id="freeTextContainer8949958723841176974">My first thought when I started reading this book was that I couldn’t relate to the 1st-person characters at all. And then I realized that he’s a complete sociopath. Back in my 20s, I had a very close friend that was kinda like this guy. Okay, okay. I was sorta kinda dating this guy. But he finally crossed a social and sanity line, and I just had to walk away. I started to realize that the things he joked about he was secretly serious about. But OMG. This is book is like stepping into that guys </span>
  <span id="freeText8949958723841176974" style="display:none">My first thought when I started reading this book was that I couldn’t relate to the 1st-person characters at all. And then I realized that he’s a complete sociopath. Back in my 20s, I had a very close friend that was kinda like this guy. Okay, okay. I was sorta kinda dating this guy. But he finally crossed a social and sanity line, and I just had to walk away. I started to realize that the things he joked about he was secretly serious about. But OMG. This is book is like stepping into that guys mind in some ways. I’m not sure what the plot of this book is beyond this teenage kid, Oliver, trying to figure out his love life and trying to catch his parents cheating on each other. But I couldn’t look away because this guy’s mind works so counter to my own. <br /><br />Anyhow, I’ll leave you with some random quotes:<br /><br />“I like the way beetroot turns your wee pinkish red; I like to pretend that I have internal bleeding.”<br /><br />“She’s watching my father with a familiar expression—a mixture of disgust and affection—that she adopts when she sees me use my own earwax as lip gloss. I believe in recycling.”<br /><br />“I would never say snog. I would say osculate.”<br /><br />“I rip open a condom packet. The smell of no child support.”<br /><br />“I preferred her when she was pure, untapped potential: a sex kitten stuck in the belly of an orca.”<br /><br />Anyhow, there’s a movie version of the book that I think I’m going to have to rent just because I need to see how someone interprets this weirdness in film. </span>

        <a title="Meghan Schuyler" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Meghan Schuyler" href="/user/show/18604124-meghan-schuyler">Meghan Schuyler</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780812978391" href="/book/show/7715769-submarine">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer889267649" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10571614523818755883">(4.5 stars) This was a superb piece of literature. It was just the right dose of quirky without being pretentious, and it was hilarious without exploiting that factor. I was extremely amused during this entire novel, and though not a lot occurred it was compelling nonetheless. I have seen a few reviews that mentioned this, and have to agree that it is resemblant of The Catcher in the Rye with it's witty, troubled, male protagonist and somewhat lacking, yet gripping, plot. I will additionally app</span>
  <span id="freeText10571614523818755883" style="display:none">(4.5 stars) This was a superb piece of literature. It was just the right dose of quirky without being pretentious, and it was hilarious without exploiting that factor. I was extremely amused during this entire novel, and though not a lot occurred it was compelling nonetheless. I have seen a few reviews that mentioned this, and have to agree that it is resemblant of The Catcher in the Rye with it's witty, troubled, male protagonist and somewhat lacking, yet gripping, plot. I will additionally applaud the ending, whereas while so many teen novels, and novels in general have a "happy-ending," where everything comes out the way it should, this novel strayed from that aspect a bit. Although I often read novels to escape from my somewhat dull existence, and consequently seek the sort of solace a happy-ending brings, it was refreshing for some things to not completely work out by the end of this book, that typically would have if it were written by another author. As always, I would not even compare the movie to the book, in fact I would advise not watching the movie at all, as it does not even really come close to the comedic genius the book provided. I most definitely intend to recommend this book to friends, and I look forward to reading more of Joe Dunthorne's work. </span>
  <a data-text-id="10571614523818755883" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_284067307" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/284067307" />
    <a title="Cristy" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3779025-cristy"><img alt="Cristy" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1547601303p2/3779025.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/284067307?book_show_action=true">Feb 25, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Cristy" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Cristy" href="/user/show/3779025-cristy">Cristy</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/6646824-submarine">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3779025-cristy?shelf=coming-of-age">coming-of-age</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3779025-cristy?shelf=humorous">humorous</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3779025-cristy?shelf=cult-classic">cult-classic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3779025-cristy?shelf=kindle">kindle</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer284067307" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11442290565109922149">I was in was in the mood for something that would make me laugh and for the most part, this read did the trick. Joe Dunthorne’s SUBMARINE is not the usual coming of age tale. To one extent Oliver Tate, of South Wales, is intelligent, awkward, funny, weird and quirky which is kind of typical of an adolescent protagonist character; but on the other hand he was so much more… I don’t know, off maybe? In fact quite unexpectedly, Oliver was a bit intense for someone his age, an over thinker and actual</span>
  <span id="freeText11442290565109922149" style="display:none">I was in was in the mood for something that would make me laugh and for the most part, this read did the trick. Joe Dunthorne’s SUBMARINE is not the usual coming of age tale. To one extent Oliver Tate, of South Wales, is intelligent, awkward, funny, weird and quirky which is kind of typical of an adolescent protagonist character; but on the other hand he was so much more… I don’t know, off maybe? In fact quite unexpectedly, Oliver was a bit intense for someone his age, an over thinker and actually somewhat pompous and mean to an extent. And even thought SUBMARINE has been genre as a coming of age story, of which in many ways it is just exactly that, but it does lack a “lessons learned” or growth from the main character that you normally expect from this type of book. Oliver pretty much is who he is; take it or leave it, these are his experiences. All that said, to Dunthorne’s credit, the young protagonist was still surprisingly “likable”. <br /><br />Entertaining, candid and slightly disturbing, SUBMARINE has a dark humor that is a perfect fit for fans of great novels such as Colin Higgins’s cult classic, Harold and Maude.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="11442290565109922149" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_500934320" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/500934320" />
    <a title="Andrew Woods" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/16260255-andrew-woods"><img alt="Andrew Woods" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1357822755p2/16260255.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/500934320?book_show_action=true">Jan 09, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Andrew Woods" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Andrew Woods" href="/user/show/16260255-andrew-woods">Andrew Woods</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer500934320" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15830532190780649451">I was introduced to the novel through Ayoade's magnificent film adaptation. But the book met my fairly high expectations! I laughed throughout. Dunthorne has a talent for description and similes. The protagonist, Oliver, is an introspective, intelligent 16 year old who struggles to understand the motives and desires of others. He consistently fails in his attempts of psychologically adjusting his parents and girlfriend to how he feels the world should be arranged. <br/>Oliver is a submarine that want</span>
  <span id="freeText15830532190780649451" style="display:none">I was introduced to the novel through Ayoade's magnificent film adaptation. But the book met my fairly high expectations! I laughed throughout. Dunthorne has a talent for description and similes. The protagonist, Oliver, is an introspective, intelligent 16 year old who struggles to understand the motives and desires of others. He consistently fails in his attempts of psychologically adjusting his parents and girlfriend to how he feels the world should be arranged. <br />Oliver is a submarine that wants the sea to move for him. <br />The narrative diverts through various mediums; straight storytelling, pamphlets, diaries, emails. All these methods of communication only distance Oliver from interacting truthfully with those around him; will he ever achieve this? <br />The book and the film end differently. <br />I prefer the book. </span>
  <a data-text-id="15830532190780649451" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3370732933" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3370732933" />
    <a title="Anna Ward-Gow" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/10820789-anna-ward-gow"><img alt="Anna Ward-Gow" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1593484238p2/10820789.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3370732933?book_show_action=true">Jun 02, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Anna Ward-Gow" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Anna Ward-Gow" href="/user/show/10820789-anna-ward-gow">Anna Ward-Gow</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3370732933" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4499668189037079948">This was a rather painful read and having just seen a review comparing it to The Catcher in the Rye- well! I had to find a chair before I fainted. <br/><br/>The most glaring disappointment was the protagonist, Oliver. Haphazardly put-together; his character lacked reasonable motivation; was jarringly precocious and unlikable. None of these aforementioned attributes were pulled-off successfully. The incessant and contrived 'quirkiness' of his character stole any hope for a readable and truly thought-provok</span>
  <span id="freeText4499668189037079948" style="display:none">This was a rather painful read and having just seen a review comparing it to The Catcher in the Rye- well! I had to find a chair before I fainted. <br /><br />The most glaring disappointment was the protagonist, Oliver. Haphazardly put-together; his character lacked reasonable motivation; was jarringly precocious and unlikable. None of these aforementioned attributes were pulled-off successfully. The incessant and contrived 'quirkiness' of his character stole any hope for a readable and truly thought-provoking bildungsroman.<br /><br />I did find the novel, on occasion, very funny but these moments turned out to only be rare glimpses of the story's unfulfilled potential. </span>
  <a data-text-id="4499668189037079948" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('7sskUWK1brlIf9Cl8eTPFu0upia/zUGHQhITYVIn85w7e/tPz7r0xUsfb66cJmzAeGPjD1IHquxV7QUT7N1FGg==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('kZSMl/4zNxZziWXlCrB6O4Z4nSKmyTVCB2AoL4sSqfdEJFOJUzytanDp2u5nctntEzXYC0sD3ikQnz5dNegfcQ==')})
  }

Dominating the world around us, from Trump\'s twitter outbursts to deadly gun violence, from male suicide rates to incels on Reddit and 4chan, masculinity is perceived to be \'toxic\', \'fragile\' and \'in crisis\'. In Mask Off, JJ Bola exposes masculinity as a performance that men are socially conditioned into. Using examples of non-Western cultural traditions, music and sport, he shines light on historical narratives around manhood, debunking popular myths along the way. He explores how LGBTQ men, men of colour, and male refugees experience masculinity in diverse ways, revealing its fluidity, how it\'s strengthened and weakened by different political contexts, such as the patriarchy or the far-right, and perceived differently by those around them. At the heart of love and sex, the political stage, competitive sports, gang culture, and mental health issues, lies masculinity: Mask Off is an urgent call to unravel masculinity and redefine Burroughs, es al mismo tiempo un descarnado autorretrato narrativo, una historia de amor brutalmente realista, una grotesca fantasía tragicómica y una ingeniosa novela política. Un libro que proporciona muchas claves fundamentales para adentrarse en el arrollador universo literario del autor. Esta edición definitiva, editada con motivo del 25 aniversario de su primera publicación, incorpora una extensa y documentada introducción de Oliver Harris en la que se repasan las complejas circunstancias personales en las que la obra fue escrita y que marcarían la vida y la posterior trayectoria literaria de Burroughs. Un álter ego del escritor, Lee, teje su tela amorosa en torno a Allerton, un joven ambiguo, indiferente como un animal. Deambula por locales cada vez más sórdidos, en los que pulula una fauna en estado de descomposición, y en esas excursiones, como un pícaro alienado, nos regala astillas radiactivas de su negrísimo humor. Para resolver sus obsesiones mortíferas y sexuales, Lee parte con su amigo a la búsqueda de la ayahuasca, droga absoluta capaz de otorgar el control total sobre los cerebros, y por eso mismo codiciada por Rusia y Estados Unidos... y por todo adicto. Dispuesto a abismarse en todos los peligros, como un santo o un criminal con orden de búsqueda y captura, Lee no tiene nada que perder. <span id="freeTextContainer17342794685714851498">Joe Dunthorne was born and brought up in Swansea, and is a graduate of the University of East Anglia's Creative Writing MA, where he was awarded the Curtis Brown prize.<br/><br/>His poetry has been published in magazines and anthologies and has featured on Channel 4, and BBC Radio 3 and 4. A pamphlet collection, Joe Dunthorne: Faber New Poets 5 was published in 2010.<br/><br/>His first novel, Submarine, the story of </span>
  <span id="freeText17342794685714851498" style="display:none">Joe Dunthorne was born and brought up in Swansea, and is a graduate of the University of East Anglia's Creative Writing MA, where he was awarded the Curtis Brown prize.<br /><br />His poetry has been published in magazines and anthologies and has featured on Channel 4, and BBC Radio 3 and 4. A pamphlet collection, Joe Dunthorne: Faber New Poets 5 was published in 2010.<br /><br />His first novel, Submarine, the story of a dysfunctional family in Swansea narrated by Oliver Tate, aged 15, was published in 2008.<br /></span>
